        <script type="text/javascript">
            function wpSolutionToggle(solution, link, showtext, hidetext) {
                if (solution.style.display != 'none') {
                    solution.style.display = 'none';
                    link.innerHTML = showtext;
                    link.className = 'solution_link_show';
                } else {
                    solution.style.display = 'block';
                    link.innerHTML = hidetext;
                    link.className = 'solution_link_hide';
                }
            }

<script type="text/javascript">
//<![CDATA[



/* JQUERY */
jQuery(document).ready(function(){ 
    
   
  

	/* jQuery('ul#rmenu').superfish(); */
	/* jQuery('ul#rmenu').superfish().find('ul').bgIframe({opacity:false}); */
 
	/* For IE6 */
	if (jQuery.browser.msie && /MSIE 6\.0/i.test(window.navigator.userAgent) && !/MSIE 7\.0/i.test(window.navigator.userAgent) && !/MSIE 8\.0/i.test(window.navigator.userAgent)) {

		/* Max-width for images in IE6 */		
		var centerwidth = jQuery("td#middle").width(); 
		
		/* Images without caption */
		jQuery(".post img").each(function() { 
			var maxwidth = centerwidth - 10 + 'px';
			var imgwidth = jQuery(this).width(); 
			var imgheight = jQuery(this).height(); 
			var newimgheight = (centerwidth / imgwidth * imgheight) + 'px';	
			if (imgwidth > centerwidth) { 
				jQuery(this).css({width: maxwidth}); 
				jQuery(this).css({height: newimgheight}); 
			}
		});
		
		/* Images with caption */
		jQuery("div.wp-caption").each(function() { 
			var captionwidth = jQuery(this).width(); 
			var maxcaptionwidth = centerwidth + 'px';
			var captionheight = jQuery(this).height();
			var captionimgwidth =  jQuery("div.wp-caption img").width();
			var captionimgheight =  jQuery("div.wp-caption img").height();
			if (captionwidth > centerwidth) { 
				jQuery(this).css({width: maxcaptionwidth}); 
				var newcaptionheight = (centerwidth / captionwidth * captionheight) + 'px';
				var newcaptionimgheight = (centerwidth / captionimgwidth * captionimgheight) + 'px';
				jQuery(this).css({height: newcaptionheight}); 
				jQuery("div.wp-caption img").css({height: newcaptionimgheight}); 
				}
		});
		
		/* sfhover for LI:HOVER support in IE6: */
		jQuery("ul li").
			hover( function() {
					jQuery(this).addClass("sfhover")
				}, 
				function() {
					jQuery(this).removeClass("sfhover")
				} 
			); 

	/* End IE6 */
	}
	
	
	
	/* Since 3.7.8: Auto resize videos (embed and iframe elements) 
	TODO: Parse parent's dimensions only once per layout column, not per video
	*/
	function bfa_resize_video() {
		jQuery('embed, iframe').each( function() {
			var video = jQuery(this),
			videoWidth = video.attr('width'); // use the attr here, not width() or css()
			videoParent = video.parent(),
			videoParentWidth = parseFloat( videoParent.css( 'width' ) ),
			videoParentBorder = parseFloat( videoParent.css( 'border-left-width' ) ) 
										+  parseFloat( videoParent.css( 'border-right-width' ) ),
			videoParentPadding = parseFloat( videoParent.css( 'padding-left' ) ) 
										+  parseFloat( videoParent.css( 'padding-right' ) ),
			maxWidth = videoParentWidth - videoParentBorder - videoParentPadding;

			if( videoWidth > maxWidth ) {
				var videoHeight = video.attr('height'),
				videoMaxHeight = ( maxWidth / videoWidth * videoHeight );
				video.attr({ width: maxWidth, height: videoMaxHeight });
			} 

		});	
	}
	bfa_resize_video();
	jQuery(window).resize( bfa_resize_video );

		
	jQuery(".post table tr").
		mouseover(function() {
			jQuery(this).addClass("over");
		}).
		mouseout(function() {
			jQuery(this).removeClass("over");
		});

	
	jQuery(".post table tr:even").
		addClass("alt");

	
	jQuery("input.text, input.TextField, input.file, input.password, textarea").
		focus(function () {  
			jQuery(this).addClass("highlight"); 
		}).
		blur(function () { 
			jQuery(this).removeClass("highlight"); 
		})
	
	jQuery("input.inputblur").
		focus(function () {  
			jQuery(this).addClass("inputfocus"); 
		}).
		blur(function () { 
			jQuery(this).removeClass("inputfocus"); 
		})

		

	
	jQuery("input.button, input.Button, input#submit").
		mouseover(function() {
			jQuery(this).addClass("buttonhover");
		}).
		mouseout(function() {
			jQuery(this).removeClass("buttonhover");
		});

	/* toggle "you can use these xhtml tags" */
	jQuery("a.xhtmltags").
		click(function(){ 
			jQuery("div.xhtml-tags").slideToggle(300); 
		});

	/* For the Tabbed Widgets plugin: */
	jQuery("ul.tw-nav-list").
		addClass("clearfix");

		
	
});

//]]>
</script>

								<div class="post-31 post type-post status-publish format-standard hentry category-cpp-tutorial odd" id="post-31">
						<div class="post-headline">		<h1>1.8 &#8212; Programs with multiple files</h1>
		</div>		<div class="post-byline">By Alex, on June 2nd, 2007</div>		<div class="post-bodycopy clearfix"><p>As programs get larger, it is not uncommon to split them into multiple files for organizational purposes.  One advantage of working with an IDE is they make working with multiple files much easier.  You already know how to create and compile single-file projects.  Adding new files to existing projects is very easy.</p>
<p><strong>Adding files to your project in Visual Studio</strong></p>
<p>In Visual Studio 2005 Express, right click on &#8220;Source Files&#8221; in the Solution Explorer window on the left, and choose Add -> New Item.  Make sure you have &#8220;C++ File (.cpp)&#8221; selected.  Give the new file a name, and it will be added to your project.</p>
<p>Note: If you create a new file from the File menu instead of from your project in the Solution Explorer, the new file won&#8217;t be added to your project automatically.  You&#8217;ll have to add it to the project manually.  To do so, right click on &#8220;Source Files&#8221; in the Solution Explorer, choose Add -> Existing Item, and then select your file.</p>
<p>When you compile your program, the new file will be automatically included, since it&#8217;s part of your project.</p>
<p><strong>Adding files to your project in Code::Blocks</strong></p>
<p>In Code::Blocks, go to the file menu and choose &#8220;new file&#8221;.  Give the new file a name (don&#8217;t forget the .cpp extension), and Code::Blocks will ask you if you want to add it to the active project.  Click &#8220;Yes&#8221;.  Note that you will also have to click the &#8220;Release&#8221; and &#8220;Debug&#8221; checkboxes, to make sure it gets added to both versions.</p>
<p>If Code::Blocks doesn&#8217;t ask you whether you want to add the file to the active project, or if the file doesn&#8217;t show up in the project pane, you&#8217;ll need to add it to the project manually.  To do so, right click on the project name in the project pane, choose &#8220;Add File&#8221;, locate the file you just created, and add it to the project.</p>
<p>Now when you compile your program, the new file will be automatically included, since it&#8217;s part of your project.</p>
<p>If the new file isn&#8217;t compiling or linking with Code::Blocks, make sure the file is set to compile and link.  To do so, right click on the file in the project pane, and choose &#8220;Properties&#8221;.  Under the &#8220;General&#8221; tab, you should see checkboxes that control whether the file is compiled and linked.  Make sure they are checked.</p>
<p><strong>Adding files to your project from the command line</strong></p>
<p>From the command line, you can create the additional file yourself, using your favorite editor, and give it a name.  When you compile your program, you&#8217;ll need to include all of the relevant code files on the compile line.  For example: &#8220;g++ main.cpp add.cpp -o main&#8221;, where main.cpp and add.cpp are the names of your code files, and main is the name of the output file.</p>
<p><strong>A multi-file example</strong></p>
<p>Now, consider the following multiple-file program:</p>
<p>add.cpp:</p><!-- Crayon Syntax Highlighter v2.7.1 -->

				</td>
						<td class="crayon-code"><div class="crayon-pre" style="font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important; -moz-tab-size:4; -o-tab-size:4; -webkit-tab-size:4; tab-size:4;"><div class="crayon-line" id="crayon-555d73603a95a770862554-1"><span class="crayon-c">//#include "stdafx.h" // uncomment if using Visual Studio</span></div><div class="crayon-line crayon-striped-line" id="crayon-555d73603a95a770862554-2">&nbsp;</div><div class="crayon-line" id="crayon-555d73603a95a770862554-3"><span class="crayon-t">int</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-e">add</span><span class="crayon-sy">(</span><span class="crayon-t">int</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-v">x</span><span class="crayon-sy">,</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-t">int</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-v">y</span><span class="crayon-sy">)</span></div><div class="crayon-line crayon-striped-line" id="crayon-555d73603a95a770862554-4"><span class="crayon-sy">{</span></div><div class="crayon-line" id="crayon-555d73603a95a770862554-5"><span class="crayon-h">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</span><span class="crayon-st">return</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-v">x</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-o">+</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-v">y</span><span class="crayon-sy">;</span></div><div class="crayon-line crayon-striped-line" id="crayon-555d73603a95a770862554-6"><span class="crayon-sy">}</span></div></div></td>
					</tr>
				</table>
			</div>
		</div>
<!-- [Format Time: 0.0009 seconds] -->
<p></p>
<p>main.cpp:</p><!-- Crayon Syntax Highlighter v2.7.1 -->

		<div id="crayon-555d73603a96a569411956" class="crayon-syntax crayon-theme-familiar-learncppcom crayon-font-monaco crayon-os-pc print-yes notranslate" data-settings=" no-popup minimize scroll-mouseover wrap" style=" margin-top: 12px; margin-bottom: 12px; font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;">

<!-- [Format Time: 0.0012 seconds] -->
<p></p>
<p>Try compiling this program for yourself.  You will note that it doesn&#8217;t compile, and it gives the same compiler error as the program in the previous lesson where the functions were declared in the wrong order:</p>
<pre>
add.cpp(10) : error C3861: &#039;add&#039;: identifier not found
add.cpp(15) : error C2365: &#039;add&#039; : redefinition; previous definition was &#039;formerly unknown identifier&#039;
</pre>
<p>When the compiler is compiling a code file, it does not know about the existence of functions that live in any other files.  This limited visibility is intentional, so that files may have functions or variables that have the same names as those in other files without causing a naming conflict.</p>
<p>However, in this case, we want main.cpp to know about (and use) the add() function that lives in add.cpp.  To give main.cpp access to the add function, we can use a forward declaration:</p>
<p>main.cpp with forward declaration:</p><!-- Crayon Syntax Highlighter v2.7.1 -->

		<div id="crayon-555d73603a972711421566" class="crayon-syntax crayon-theme-familiar-learncppcom crayon-font-monaco crayon-os-pc print-yes notranslate" data-settings=" no-popup minimize scroll-mouseover wrap" style=" margin-top: 12px; margin-bottom: 12px; font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;">
		
			<div class="crayon-plain-wrap"></div>
			<div class="crayon-main" style="">
				<table class="crayon-table">
					<tr class="crayon-row">
				<td class="crayon-nums " data-settings="show">
					<div class="crayon-nums-content" style="font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;"><div class="crayon-num" data-line="crayon-555d73603a972711421566-1">1</div><div class="crayon-num crayon-striped-num" data-line="crayon-555d73603a972711421566-2">2</div><div class="crayon-num" data-line="crayon-555d73603a972711421566-3">3</div><div class="crayon-num crayon-striped-num" data-line="crayon-555d73603a972711421566-4">4</div><div class="crayon-num" data-line="crayon-555d73603a972711421566-5">5</div><div class="crayon-num crayon-striped-num" data-line="crayon-555d73603a972711421566-6">6</div><div class="crayon-num" data-line="crayon-555d73603a972711421566-7">7</div><div class="crayon-num crayon-striped-num" data-line="crayon-555d73603a972711421566-8">8</div><div class="crayon-num" data-line="crayon-555d73603a972711421566-9">9</div><div class="crayon-num crayon-striped-num" data-line="crayon-555d73603a972711421566-10">10</div><div class="crayon-num" data-line="crayon-555d73603a972711421566-11">11</div></div>
				</td>
						<td class="crayon-code"><div class="crayon-pre" style="font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important; -moz-tab-size:4; -o-tab-size:4; -webkit-tab-size:4; tab-size:4;"><div class="crayon-line" id="crayon-555d73603a972711421566-1"><span class="crayon-c">//#include "stdafx.h" // uncomment if using Visual Studio</span></div><div class="crayon-line crayon-striped-line" id="crayon-555d73603a972711421566-2"><span class="crayon-p">#include &lt;iostream&gt;</span></div><div class="crayon-line" id="crayon-555d73603a972711421566-3">&nbsp;</div><div class="crayon-line crayon-striped-line" id="crayon-555d73603a972711421566-4"><span class="crayon-t">int</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-e">add</span><span class="crayon-sy">(</span><span class="crayon-t">int</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-v">x</span><span class="crayon-sy">,</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-t">int</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-v">y</span><span class="crayon-sy">)</span><span class="crayon-sy">;</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-c">// needed so main.cpp knows that add() is a function declared elsewhere</span></div><div class="crayon-line" id="crayon-555d73603a972711421566-5">&nbsp;</div><div class="crayon-line crayon-striped-line" id="crayon-555d73603a972711421566-6"><span class="crayon-t">int</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-e">main</span><span class="crayon-sy">(</span><span class="crayon-sy">)</span></div><div class="crayon-line" id="crayon-555d73603a972711421566-7"><span class="crayon-sy">{</span></div><div class="crayon-line crayon-striped-line" id="crayon-555d73603a972711421566-8"><span class="crayon-h">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</span><span class="crayon-r">using</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-t">namespace</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-v">std</span><span class="crayon-sy">;</span></div><div class="crayon-line" id="crayon-555d73603a972711421566-9"><span class="crayon-h">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</span><span class="crayon-r">cout</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-o">&lt;&lt;</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-s">"The sum of 3 and 4 is: "</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-o">&lt;&lt;</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-e">add</span><span class="crayon-sy">(</span><span class="crayon-cn">3</span><span class="crayon-sy">,</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-cn">4</span><span class="crayon-sy">)</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-o">&lt;&lt;</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-v">endl</span><span class="crayon-sy">;</span></div><div class="crayon-line crayon-striped-line" id="crayon-555d73603a972711421566-10"><span class="crayon-h">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</span><span class="crayon-st">return</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-cn">0</span><span class="crayon-sy">;</span></div><div class="crayon-line" id="crayon-555d73603a972711421566-11"><span class="crayon-sy">}</span></div></div></td>
					</tr>
				</table>
			</div>
		</div>
<!-- [Format Time: 0.0016 seconds] -->
<p></p>
<p>Now, when the compiler is compiling main.cpp, it will know what add() is.  Using this method, we can give files access to functions that live in another file.</p>
<p>Try compiling add.cpp and the main.cpp with the forward declaration for yourself.  If you get a linker error, make sure you&#8217;ve added add.cpp to your project or compilation line properly.</p>
<p><strong>Something went wrong!</strong></p>
<p>There are plenty of things that can go wrong the first time you try to work with multiple files.  If you tried the above example and ran into an error, check the following:</p>
<p>1. If you get a compiler error about add() not being defined in main(), you probably forgot the forward declaration in main.</p>
<p>2. If you get a linker error about add not being defined, e.g.</p>
<pre>unresolved external symbol "int __cdecl add(int,int)" (?add@@YAHHH@Z) referenced in function _main</pre>
<p>2a. &#8230;the most likely reason is that add.cpp is not added to your project correctly.  If you&#8217;re using Visual Studio or Code::Blocks, you should see add.cpp listed in the Solution Explorer/project pane on the left side of the IDE.  If you don&#8217;t, right click on your project, and add the file, then try compiling again.  If you&#8217;re compiling on the command line, don&#8217;t forget to include both main.cpp and add.cpp in your compile command.</p>
<p>2b. &#8230;it&#8217;s possible that you added add.cpp to the wrong project.</p>
<p>2c. &#8230;it&#8217;s possible that the file is set to not compile or link.  Check the file properties and ensure the file is configured to be compiled/linked.  In Code::Blocks, compile and link are separate checkboxes that should be checked.  In Visual Studio, there&#8217;s an &#8220;exclude from build&#8221; option that should be set to &#8220;no&#8221; or left blank.</p>
<p>3. If you&#8217;re using Visual Studio with precompiled headers, <em>every</em> code file needs to #include &#8220;stdafx.h&#8221;.  This includes both main.cpp and add.cpp.</p>
<p>4. Do <em>not</em> #include &#8220;add.cpp&#8221; from main.cpp.  This will cause the compiler to insert the contents of add.cpp directly into main.cpp instead of treating them as separate files.  While it may compile and run for this simple example, you will encounter problems down the road using this method.</p>
<p><strong>Conclusion</strong></p>
<p>We will begin working with multiple files a lot once we get into object-oriented programming, so now&#8217;s as good a time as any to make sure you understand how to add and compile multiple file projects.</p>

		February 18, 2015 at 8:49 am</a>
        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/18-programs-with-multiple-files/?replytocom=251553#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-251553", "251553", "respond", "31" )' aria-label='Reply to Catreece'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>Yay, I was correct then! I just added it out of reflex because I assumed that the computer would be too stupid to know what to do with anything if not specifically hand-fed the instructions in every single case.</p>
<p>I was not disappointed. =P</p>
<p>Still, I can see that being handy. It&apos;d mean that individual files can each reference specific different libraries, which probably cuts down on overlap of names by a large margin. If the audio and video libraries have overlap, but you have a video function and an audio function both as their own, separate files, then each individual file can only load up the libraries they specifically need to run, and there won&apos;t be a naming conflict that way.</p>
<p>I&apos;m starting to think the motto for C++ should be &quot;trust the programmer, but only trust whoever wrote the library as far as you can throw them.&quot;</p>
		</div>

		<li class="comment even thread-even depth-1 clearfix" id="comment-38770">
		<div id="div-comment-38770" class="clearfix comment-container">
		<div class="comment-author vcard">
		<img alt='' src='http://2.gravatar.com/avatar/858f626b3e5a0bf5d742db7b0e422be6?s=65&#038;d=mm&#038;r=g' srcset='http://2.gravatar.com/avatar/858f626b3e5a0bf5d742db7b0e422be6?s=130&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x' class='avatar avatar-65 photo' height='65' width='65' />		<span class="authorname">David</span>
		</div>
				<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
		<a href="http://www.learncpp.com/cpp-tutorial/18-programs-with-multiple-files/#comment-38770">
		January 22, 2009 at 3:48 pm</a>
        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/18-programs-with-multiple-files/?replytocom=38770#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-38770", "38770", "respond", "31" )' aria-label='Reply to David'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>These two statements you have made don&#8217;t seem to agree:</p>
<p>In the tutorial:<br />
&#8220;When the compiler is compiling a code file, it does not know about the existence of functions that live in any other files. [this sentence-&gt;] This is done so that files may have functions or variables that have the same names as those in other files without causing a conflict.&#8221;</p>
<p>In your comments:<br />
&#8220;If you have the same function declared in multiple files and you try and link those files into the same project, the linker will complain that it doesnt know which version of add() to use.&#8221;</p>
<p>So which is it? If the compiler complains about having multiple files in the project containing the same function names, then your first statement (tutorial statement) is not true -- there is in fact a conflict.</p>
		</div>
<ul class="children">
		<li class="comment byuser comment-author-alex bypostauthor odd alt depth-2 clearfix" id="comment-41988">
		<div id="div-comment-41988" class="clearfix comment-container bypostauthor">
		<div class="comment-author vcard">
		<img alt='' src='http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/a4e62d28c29f6c864923207d4c17993b?s=65&#038;d=mm&#038;r=g' srcset='http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/a4e62d28c29f6c864923207d4c17993b?s=130&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x' class='avatar avatar-65 photo' height='65' width='65' />		<span class="authorname"><a href='http://www.tarindel.com' rel='external nofollow' class='url'>Alex</a></span>
		</div>
				<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
		<a href="http://www.learncpp.com/cpp-tutorial/18-programs-with-multiple-files/#comment-41988">
		February 5, 2009 at 11:37 pm</a>
        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/18-programs-with-multiple-files/?replytocom=41988#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-41988", "41988", "respond", "31" )' aria-label='Reply to Alex'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>Both statements are true.</p>
<p>The <i>compiler</i> does not know about functions that are defined in other files (unless you use a prototype).</p>
<p>However, if you have functions with duplicate names in different files (which the compiler will not complain about), the <i>linker</i> will complain, because it doesn&#8217;t know which one to resolve the function call to.</p>
		</div>
</li><!-- #comment-## -->
</ul><!-- .children -->

		</div>
		<p>`Circular xxx &lt;- yyy dependency dropped.&#8217;<br />
This means that make detected a loop in the dependency graph: after tracing the prerequisite yyy of target xxx, and its prerequisites, etc., one of them depended on xxx again. </p>
<p>Maybe you have included the cpp files and made a loop or something.</p>

		<p>Hi.</p>
<p>I was trying to declare some struct types and enum types along with a few variables (of those types)in a separate file. In the header file I&#8217;ve just included declaration of the variables with the extern keyword. (Yes, I&#8217;m using global variables, but I&#8217;m new to C++ and i couldn&#8217;t figure out any other way. Besides it&#8217;s not that big of a program to be that difficult to track down the variables.) But when the compiler gets to</p>
<p>#include &#8220;buildings.h&#8221; //the header file</p>
<p>it gives an error because it doesn&#8217;t recognize the structs I&#8217;ve declared in the separate file as types. If I declare the types in the main file before the line</p>
<p>#include &#8220;buildings.h&#8221;</p>
<p>it&#8217;s all OK, but my idea was to keep the whole program somewhat clean so the types declarations and the variables of that types to be in the same separate file.</p>
<p>Is there something I could do to make the whole project easier to understand by keeping the variables and their custom made types (structs, enums)in one file and each function in a separate file, without having to crowd the main file code with type declarations?</p>

		<p>It should probably be mentioned here that #include &#8220;stdafx.h&#8221; should be placed at the top of each file as the program did not compile without it.  I had assumed incorrectly that the compiler would simply include whatever was on the main function file for each file.  I&#8217;m assuming if you want to include anything else it must be placed at the top of each file.</p>

		<p>I found out why it isnt running on codeblocks (atleast on my computer). When you open a new file for the project, it creates the file with just *.c instead of *.cpp. Try saving the new &#8220;add&#8221; file with .cpp at the end. I did that and it ran with no errors</p>

		<li class="comment odd alt depth-3 clearfix" id="comment-207424">
		<div id="div-comment-207424" class="clearfix comment-container">
		<div class="comment-author vcard">
		<img alt='' src='http://2.gravatar.com/avatar/2dc58df301aaf9474e1d1a1a14608917?s=65&#038;d=mm&#038;r=g' srcset='http://2.gravatar.com/avatar/2dc58df301aaf9474e1d1a1a14608917?s=130&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x' class='avatar avatar-65 photo' height='65' width='65' />		<span class="authorname">rameye</span>
		</div>
				<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
		<a href="http://www.learncpp.com/cpp-tutorial/18-programs-with-multiple-files/#comment-207424">
		November 1, 2014 at 9:52 pm</a>
        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/18-programs-with-multiple-files/?replytocom=207424#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-207424", "207424", "respond", "31" )' aria-label='Reply to rameye'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>You can do it that way, but that defeats the purpose of a multiple file build. Using #include just makes the preprocessor copy and paste the contents of a different file into main.cpp, and main.cpp will still be the only file compiled afterward.</p>
<p>What you need to do is make your IDE aware of the second file by adding it into the project you have open, which previously was only a single file project. When you create the second file you need to do it using the IDE &#8220;New file&#8221; and select &#8220;Add to project&#8221; or something relevant to that. If your IDE shows a tree structure of project files, your new file should then show up in it.</p>
<p>When you do this you make the IDE configure itself to use the new file when it builds the project into an executable.</p>
<p>I use NetBeans but I think all IDE&#8217;s work the same in this regard, although the terms they use in menus and such may be different from one to another.</p>
		</div>
</li><!-- #comment-## -->
</ul><!-- .children -->

						<td class="crayon-code"><div class="crayon-pre" style="font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important; -moz-tab-size:4; -o-tab-size:4; -webkit-tab-size:4; tab-size:4;"><div class="crayon-line" id="crayon-555d73603f0e8575075998-1"><span class="crayon-p">#include &lt;stdafx.h&gt;</span></div><div class="crayon-line crayon-striped-line" id="crayon-555d73603f0e8575075998-2">&nbsp;</div><div class="crayon-line" id="crayon-555d73603f0e8575075998-3"><span class="crayon-t">int</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-e">add</span><span class="crayon-sy">(</span><span class="crayon-t">int</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-v">x</span><span class="crayon-sy">,</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-t">int</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-v">y</span><span class="crayon-sy">)</span><span class="crayon-sy">{</span></div><div class="crayon-line crayon-striped-line" id="crayon-555d73603f0e8575075998-4"><span class="crayon-h">	</span><span class="crayon-st">return</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-v">x</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-o">+</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-v">y</span><span class="crayon-sy">;</span></div><div class="crayon-line" id="crayon-555d73603f0e8575075998-5"><span class="crayon-sy">}</span></div></div></td>
					</tr>
				</table>
			</div>
		</div>
<!-- [Format Time: 0.0008 seconds] -->
<p></p>
<p>It doesn&#8217;t seem to work without the stdafx.h file in add.cpp, is that right?</p>

		<p>I&#8217;m using Code::Blocks 12.11 and I had the same problem. The problem was that the add.cpp file was not added to the C::B project(.cbp file) although it was created with the C::B editor.</p>
<p>One solution:<br />
Right click on the project in the editor and select &#8220;Add files&#8221;. After that navigate to the project&#8217;s folder and select &#8220;add.cpp&#8221; and click Open.</p>
<p>Hope this might help someone dealing with the same issue in the future.</p>

		<p>I have a question. How does the compiler know what to pick add.cpp. I am learning C++ and I&#8217;ve learned that you need forward declarations in any and all situations. Multiple files or not. At the top of the code are the declarations (prototypes), then the main file which utilizes functions, then after the main you include definitions of functions. The compiler goes in order, first sees the prototypes and knows the function is defined somewhere and then goes to the main, when that function is called the compiler proceeds through the source code until it finds a function with a matching name and parameter list. It then carries out that function with the parameters passed to it in main. </p>
<p>What does the compiler do when the function is not defined in the same source code as its call and prototype. Does it look in the current directory for source code files with the same name as the function? Does it look at all the .cpp files in the current directory for anything that has a function with the same name and parameter list? By what magic does the compiler know what file contains the function definition when it leaves the source code that calls the function?</p>

		<li class="comment odd alt depth-2 clearfix" id="comment-105795">
		<div id="div-comment-105795" class="clearfix comment-container">
		<div class="comment-author vcard">
		<img alt='' src='http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/df4dbff02a05bfad8d0411bdc2664f38?s=65&#038;d=mm&#038;r=g' srcset='http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/df4dbff02a05bfad8d0411bdc2664f38?s=130&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x' class='avatar avatar-65 photo' height='65' width='65' />		<span class="authorname">numberneyen</span>
		</div>
				<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
		<a href="http://www.learncpp.com/cpp-tutorial/18-programs-with-multiple-files/#comment-105795">
		September 25, 2013 at 12:28 pm</a>
        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/18-programs-with-multiple-files/?replytocom=105795#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-105795", "105795", "respond", "31" )' aria-label='Reply to numberneyen'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>Hi I&#8217;m brand new to C++, but I did have the same question, and with some messing around this is what I&#8217;ve found to be apparently the case, although this is merely from observation and not from any teaching or really foundational knowledge (I could be wrong):</p>
<p>Also be aware that I&#8217;m not 100% if the error is with the compiler or the linker, so while I write &#8220;compile&#8221;, it may actually be a linking issue. Still, I give a little solution ?</p>
<p>The project won&#8217;t compile if there are multiple functions of the same name with the same number/type of parameters in any of the files. For example, let&#8217;s say you have main.cpp which includes the function &#8220;int add(int x, int y)&#8221; and addxz.cpp which includes the function &#8220;int add(int x, int z)&#8221; in it. Despite the different names given the parameters inside the function in addxz.cpp, it is an int function named add() which requires two parameters, and so the project will not compile; there is no magic way for the compiler to tell the difference between the two functions. The solution? More specific names, i.e. call the function in addxz.cpp &#8220;int addxz(int x, int z)&#8221;. This clarifies the code for both the reader and the compiler.</p>
		</div>
</li><!-- #comment-## -->
		<li class="comment even depth-2 clearfix" id="comment-207521">
		<div id="div-comment-207521" class="clearfix comment-container">
		<div class="comment-author vcard">
		<img alt='' src='http://2.gravatar.com/avatar/2dc58df301aaf9474e1d1a1a14608917?s=65&#038;d=mm&#038;r=g' srcset='http://2.gravatar.com/avatar/2dc58df301aaf9474e1d1a1a14608917?s=130&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x' class='avatar avatar-65 photo' height='65' width='65' />		<span class="authorname">rameye</span>
		</div>
				<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
		<a href="http://www.learncpp.com/cpp-tutorial/18-programs-with-multiple-files/#comment-207521">
		November 1, 2014 at 11:21 pm</a>
        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/18-programs-with-multiple-files/?replytocom=207521#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-207521", "207521", "respond", "31" )' aria-label='Reply to rameye'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>Think of the IDE as a machine that keeps track of a collection of source files that need to be compiled and linked together into one executable.</p>
<p>The collection of source files along with project configuration files and generated &#8216;make&#8217; files is what is called the project.</p>
<p>The compiler and linker that is used by the IDE is whatever you told it to use when you initally setup the IDE. The compiler and linker are separate runnable programs on their own, you <b>can</b> use them from a command line without ever using an IDE in the first place.</p>
<p>What the IDE does is automate for you all the typing that you yourself would have to do on the command line to compile and link all those files, and in a big project with lots and lots of source files buried many directories deep, that would quickly become tedious and prone to error every time you added, edited, or removed a source file from your project.</p>
<p>The IDE also keeps track of where to put everything neatly on your storage device, and remembers for you where to look again for them.</p>
<p>What happens when you &#8220;build&#8221; the project that is opened in the IDE?</p>
<p>The IDE runs a &#8216;make&#8217; file that it had previously generated for the project, and that &#8216;make&#8217; file runs the compiler and linker for you, compiling all the source files and then linking all the object files together into one beautiful executable file.</p>
<p>The linking step is where functions that live in one file but are called from another file are resolved.</p>
<p>Compilers and linkers can work with multiple files, the files are just specified as separate arguments when calling the compiler or linker.</p>
<p>For example, the C++ compiler my IDE uses is <b>g++</b> and the commands my IDE used to compile and link both main.cpp and add.cpp were:</p>
<p><b>g++    -c -O2 -MMD -MP -MF &#8220;build/Release/GNU-Linux-x86/add.o.d&#8221; -o build/Release/GNU-Linux-x86/add.o add.cpp</p>
<p>g++    -c -O2 -MMD -MP -MF &#8220;build/Release/GNU-Linux-x86/main.o.d&#8221; -o build/Release/GNU-Linux-x86/main.o main.cpp</p>
<p>g++     -o dist/Release/GNU-Linux-x86/cppapplication_1 build/Release/GNU-Linux-x86/add.o build/Release/GNU-Linux-x86/main.o</b></p>
<p>The first command compiled the file add.cpp and created a file named add.o (An object file)</p>
<p>The second command compiled the file main.cpp and created a file named main.o (Another object file)</p>
<p>The third command told the compiler to use a linker to link both add.o and main.o into an executable file named cppapplication_1</p>
<p>So you see it is not the job of the compiler to find where the guts of functions live in multiple .cpp files, it is the job of the linker. The compiler makes the guts, the linker finds the guts. All the compiler needs is to be assured by your forward declaration that the function has indeed been fully defined somewhere in one of the .cpp files you specified to it, and it will leave a note in the object file that it generates for the linker (that note being the function signature from the forward declaration), the linker then picks it up and goes looking in the object files for the correct function guts, and places the guts correctly as machine code in the executable file it is constructing. In the spot that you called the function within your C++ source file.</p>
<p>Beautiful isn&#8217;t it?</p>
		</div>
<ul class="children">
		<li class="comment odd alt depth-3 clearfix" id="comment-207540">

        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/18-programs-with-multiple-files/?replytocom=207540#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-207540", "207540", "respond", "31" )' aria-label='Reply to rameye'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>When I said &#8220;places the guts correctly as machine code in the executable file it is constructing&#8221; I meant that it places the machine code for a call of the function, not the machine code of the function. The machine code of the function itself will reside in it&#8217;s own spot elsewhere in the executable. Unless you use the <b><code>inline</code></b> keyword with the function declaration. But that&#8217;s another subject totally.</p>
		</div>

		September 2, 2012 at 7:20 pm</a>
        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/18-programs-with-multiple-files/?replytocom=97779#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-97779", "97779", "respond", "31" )' aria-label='Reply to neuronet'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>I got it to work just by including the usual headers that were not included in the code snippets in the original post. Let me know if it doesn&#8217;t work, I&#8217;m sure I won&#8217;t have any idea why&#8230; <img src="http://www.learncpp.com/blog/wp-includes/images/smilies/simple-smile.png" alt=":)" class="wp-smiley" style="height: 1em; max-height: 1em;" /></p>
<p>main.cpp:<br />
//First attempt at a project with function defined separately<br />
#include<br />
#include<br />
#include<br />
using namespace std;</p>
<p>int add_two(int in1, int in2); //function prototype</p>
<p>//Main<br />
int main(int nNumberofArgs, char* pszArgs[])<br />
{<br />
        int in1;<br />
        int in2;<br />
        int sum;</p>
<p>        cout &lt;&lt; &quot;Function Test\n&quot;;<br />
        cout &lt;&gt; in1;<br />
        cout &lt;&gt; in2;<br />
        cout &lt;&lt; endl;</p>
<p>        sum=add_two(in1, in2);<br />
        cout &lt;&lt; &quot;Sum of your guys is : &quot; &lt;&lt; sum &lt;&lt; endl;<br />
        return 0;<br />
}</p>
<p>add_two.cpp:<br />
#include<br />
#include<br />
#include<br />
using namespace std;</p>
<p>int add_two(int firstIn, int secondIn)<br />
{<br />
 cout &lt;&lt; &quot;\nIn function \&quot;add two\&quot;\n&quot; &lt;&lt; endl;<br />
 return firstIn+secondIn;</p>
<p>}</p>
		</div>

		<li class="comment even depth-2 clearfix" id="comment-207589">
		<div id="div-comment-207589" class="clearfix comment-container">
		<div class="comment-author vcard">
		<img alt='' src='http://2.gravatar.com/avatar/2dc58df301aaf9474e1d1a1a14608917?s=65&#038;d=mm&#038;r=g' srcset='http://2.gravatar.com/avatar/2dc58df301aaf9474e1d1a1a14608917?s=130&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x' class='avatar avatar-65 photo' height='65' width='65' />		<span class="authorname">rameye</span>
		</div>
				<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
		<a href="http://www.learncpp.com/cpp-tutorial/18-programs-with-multiple-files/#comment-207589">
		November 2, 2014 at 1:06 am</a>
        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/18-programs-with-multiple-files/?replytocom=207589#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-207589", "207589", "respond", "31" )' aria-label='Reply to rameye'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>If you had no problem compiling/linking the single file examples in previous lessons then it&#8217;s not missing standard libraries causing it.</p>
<p>And yes, you need the header files even if not using an IDE. After all they are referenced in the #include directives. And without the #include directives calls to standard library functions are not going to resolve at link time.</p>
<p>All the IDE does is accomplish for you the same thing you would have to do at a command line yourself without it.</p>
<p><b><code>g++ -o myfile add.cpp main.cpp</code></b> should have compiled and then linked <b>add.cpp</b> and <b>main.cpp</b> into the executable <b>myfile</b></p>
<p>If not, then look at the first error that was given, it will point to a line # in your .cpp file. Take it from there.</p>
<p>Also try <b>g++ -help</b> or <b>g++ -v -help</b> (that&#8217;s two dashes before &#8216;help&#8217;) at the command line, I believe both will give you a list of options you can use with g++, some of them will display the paths to required c++ libraries. And holy cow, there are a ton of options for g++. Best to have an IDE to set them for you after you select human readable options in the IDE project configuration.</p>
		</div>
</li><!-- #comment-## -->

		<p>Hi, I find solution.<br />
In add.cpp, you must have<br />
<code><br />
#include &quot;stdafx.h&quot;<br />
</code><br />
at the top.</p>
<p>My add.cpp is like this:<br />
<code><br />
#include &quot;stdafx.h&quot;<br />
int add(int x,int y)<br />
{<br />
	return x+y;<br />
}<br />
</code><br />
and my main.cpp is:<br />
<code><br />
#include &quot;stdafx.h&quot;<br />
#include &lt;iostream&gt;</p>
<p>int add(int x,int y);</p>
<p>int main()<br />
{<br />
	using namespace std;<br />
	cout &lt;&lt; &quot;The sum of 3 and 4 is: &quot; &lt;&lt; add(3,4) &lt;&lt; endl;<br />
	return 0;<br />
}<br />
</code></p>
		</div>

		<p>For those having problems, I did the following:<br />
<b>main.cpp</b><br />
<code><br />
#include &quot;stdafx.h&quot;<br />
#include &lt;iostream&gt;<br />
#include &quot;add.cpp&quot;</p>
<p>int add(int x, int y); // forward declaration using function prototype</p>
<p>int main()<br />
{<br />
	using namespace std;<br />
	cout &lt;&lt; &quot;The sum of 3 and 4 is: &quot; &lt;&lt; add(3, 4) &lt;&lt; endl;<br />
	return 0;<br />
}</code></p>
<p><b>add.cpp</b><br />
<code>int add(int x, int y)<br />
{<br />
	return x + y;<br />
}</code></p>
<p>Mine compiled just fine once I included the add.cpp.</p>

		<li class="comment even depth-2 clearfix" id="comment-207623">
		<div id="div-comment-207623" class="clearfix comment-container">
		<div class="comment-author vcard">
		<img alt='' src='http://2.gravatar.com/avatar/2dc58df301aaf9474e1d1a1a14608917?s=65&#038;d=mm&#038;r=g' srcset='http://2.gravatar.com/avatar/2dc58df301aaf9474e1d1a1a14608917?s=130&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x' class='avatar avatar-65 photo' height='65' width='65' />		<span class="authorname">rameye</span>
		</div>
				<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
		<a href="http://www.learncpp.com/cpp-tutorial/18-programs-with-multiple-files/#comment-207623">
		November 2, 2014 at 1:23 am</a>
        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/18-programs-with-multiple-files/?replytocom=207623#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-207623", "207623", "respond", "31" )' aria-label='Reply to rameye'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>But you kludged it by adding the <b>#include add.cpp</b> directive.</p>
<p>The whole point here is to use multiple files when compiling, not copying one file into a spot inside another (which is what <b>#include</b> does) and then still compiling a single file.</p>
<p><b>#include</b> should only be used to insert header (.h) file contents, which contain forward declarations of external functions, not to insert other .cpp file contents.</p>
		</div>
<ul class="children">

		<img alt='' src='http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/0ac283b1c1e2c0e87bdbb29cf4d0a612?s=65&#038;d=mm&#038;r=g' srcset='http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/0ac283b1c1e2c0e87bdbb29cf4d0a612?s=130&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x' class='avatar avatar-65 photo' height='65' width='65' />		<span class="authorname">imstilllearning</span>

				<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
		<a href="http://www.learncpp.com/cpp-tutorial/18-programs-with-multiple-files/#comment-223157">
		November 21, 2014 at 7:03 am</a>
        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/18-programs-with-multiple-files/?replytocom=223157#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-223157", "223157", "respond", "31" )' aria-label='Reply to imstilllearning'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>Here is solution to those who main.cpp cannot access add.cpp.<br />
-This problem is caused by creating different source file but not in a same project.<br />
(note - I&#8217;m using dev-c++ , but you can figure this out)<br />
(note - This is for FORWARD DECLARE lesson             )</p>
<p>1. File &gt; Create new project<br />
   - Name the project MultipleFile(for example).</p>
<p>2. Create new &#8220;main&#8221; source file under this project<br />
   - Create a source file name main.cpp. </p>
<p>     // #include &#8220;stdafx.h&#8221; for those using Microsoft Visual C++</p>
<p>     #include<br />
     int add(int x, int y);<br />
     int main()<br />
     {<br />
        using namespace std;<br />
        cout &lt;&lt; &quot;The sum of 3 and 4 is: &quot; &lt;&lt; add(3, 4) &lt;&lt; endl;<br />
        return 0;<br />
     } </p>
<p>     - Compile</p>
<p>3. Create new &quot;add&quot; source file under this project.<br />
   - Create another source file name add.cpp</p>
<p>     // #include &quot;stdafx.h&quot; for those using Microsoft Visual C++</p>
<p>     #include </p>
<p>     int add(int x, int y)<br />
     {<br />
        return x + y;<br />
     }</p>
<p>    - Compile</p>
<p>4. Compile and execute &#8220;main&#8221; now, and it should work. If problem persist, try to create a new project in a new folder, as you might confused it with other &#8220;add&#8221; you made before. Or remove other source file first you made before, and then continue with this lesson.</p>
<p>Hope this help, correct me if I&#8217;m wrong.</p>
<p>p/s : So far, this website teach me a lot of things I did not notice before. Good job.</p>
		</div>

		<p>Quick question Alex, my program ran smooth as butter using two files to do so. I included the #include &quot;stdafx.h&quot;, but I&#8217;m wondering, even though it worked here just fine should I #include &lt;iostream&gt; in each file as well?</p>

<ul class="children">
		<li class="comment byuser comment-author-alex bypostauthor odd alt depth-2 clearfix" id="comment-251837">
		<div id="div-comment-251837" class="clearfix comment-container bypostauthor">
		<div class="comment-author vcard">
		<img alt='' src='http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/a4e62d28c29f6c864923207d4c17993b?s=65&#038;d=mm&#038;r=g' srcset='http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/a4e62d28c29f6c864923207d4c17993b?s=130&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x' class='avatar avatar-65 photo' height='65' width='65' />		<span class="authorname">Alex</span>
		</div>
				<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
		<a href="http://www.learncpp.com/cpp-tutorial/18-programs-with-multiple-files/#comment-251837">
		March 16, 2015 at 8:49 am</a>
        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/18-programs-with-multiple-files/?replytocom=251837#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-251837", "251837", "respond", "31" )' aria-label='Reply to Alex'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>You should only #include <iostream> if you use any of the things that iostream defines (namely, std::cout, std::cin, or std::endl).</p>
<p>If you&#8217;re not using any of these, then you don&#8217;t need to #include </iostream><iostream>.</p>
<p>One easy way to tell whether you need it is to comment out the #include.  If your program breaks, you need it. <img src="http://www.learncpp.com/blog/wp-includes/images/smilies/simple-smile.png" alt=":)" class="wp-smiley" style="height: 1em; max-height: 1em;" /></iostream></p>
		</div>
</li><!-- #comment-## -->
</ul><!-- .children -->

		<li class="comment even thread-even depth-1 clearfix" id="comment-252016">
		<div id="div-comment-252016" class="clearfix comment-container">
		<div class="comment-author vcard">
		<img alt='' src='http://2.gravatar.com/avatar/895334ff1b20bd1173dbb134925b923b?s=65&#038;d=mm&#038;r=g' srcset='http://2.gravatar.com/avatar/895334ff1b20bd1173dbb134925b923b?s=130&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x' class='avatar avatar-65 photo' height='65' width='65' />		<span class="authorname">Eason</span>

				<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
		<a href="http://www.learncpp.com/cpp-tutorial/18-programs-with-multiple-files/#comment-252016">
		April 1, 2015 at 6:06 pm</a>
        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/18-programs-with-multiple-files/?replytocom=252016#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-252016", "252016", "respond", "31" )' aria-label='Reply to Eason'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>Since the program is always starting with the main function, why there is no main function in add.cpp?</p>
		</div>
<ul class="children">
		<li class="comment byuser comment-author-alex bypostauthor odd alt depth-2 clearfix" id="comment-252034">
		<div id="div-comment-252034" class="clearfix comment-container bypostauthor">
		<div class="comment-author vcard">
		<img alt='' src='http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/a4e62d28c29f6c864923207d4c17993b?s=65&#038;d=mm&#038;r=g' srcset='http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/a4e62d28c29f6c864923207d4c17993b?s=130&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x' class='avatar avatar-65 photo' height='65' width='65' />		<span class="authorname">Alex</span>
		</div>
				<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
		<a href="http://www.learncpp.com/cpp-tutorial/18-programs-with-multiple-files/#comment-252034">
		April 2, 2015 at 4:21 pm</a>
        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/18-programs-with-multiple-files/?replytocom=252034#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-252034", "252034", "respond", "31" )' aria-label='Reply to Alex'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>Programs can only have one main() function.  For multi-file programs, such as the example above (which has add.cpp and main.cpp), main() only needs to exist in one of those files.</p>
<p>In the add example, execution starts at the main() function in main.cpp.  On line 7, it makes a call to add(), which happens to be defined in a different file (add.cpp).  Then main() returns 0 back to the operating system, and our program is finished.</p>
<p>There&#8217;s simply no need for a main() function in add.cpp.</p>
		</div>
</li><!-- #comment-## -->
</ul><!-- .children -->
